                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202312190')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e00ec347b8e84e1dbdaf2a8b0803e10e"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e00ec347b8e84e1dbdaf2a8b0803e10e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4d9a3128-c6bc-42eb-939d-79c7a187be25'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '5F6B872261A8B935F5324CBB6A42E803';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-4035-0991"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0002-4035-0991</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xiaguanglin@fudan.edu.cn"><span>xiaguanglin@fudan.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yuxuebin@fudan.edu.cn"><span>yuxuebin@fudan.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yu/Xuebin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d30669551" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Iron sulfides have attracted tremendous research interest for the anode of sodium-ion batteries due to their high capacity and abundant resource. However, the intrinsic pulverization and aggregation of iron sulfide electrodes induced by the conversion reaction during cycling are considered destructive and undesirable, which often impedes their capacity, rate capability, and long-term cycling stability. Herein, an interesting pulverization phenomenon of ultrathin carbon-coated Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S nanoplates (Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C) is observed during the first discharge process of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to the formation of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S nanoparticles with quantum size (≈5&nbsp;nm) tightly embedded in the carbon matrix. Surprisingly, no discernible aggregation phenomenon can be detected in subsequent cycles. In/ex situ experiments and theoretical calculations demonstrate that ultrafine pulverization can confer several advantages, including sustaining reversible conversion reactions, reducing the adsorption energies, and diffusion energy barriers of sodium atoms, and preventing the aggregation of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S particles by strengthening the adsorption between pulverized Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S nanoparticles and carbon. As a result, benefiting from the unique ultrafine pulverization, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C anode simultaneously exhibits high reversible capacity (610&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability (427.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and ultralong cycling stability (377.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500&nbsp;cycles at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202312190-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202312190-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently emerged as an economically promising alternative to conventional lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the cheap price and abundant natural distribution of Na resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0001_R_d30669543e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0002_R_d30669543e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Handb. Sodium-Ion Batteries">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although SIBs work on similar electrochemical mechanisms to LIBs, the development of SIBs falls far behind that of LIBs due to the intrinsic larger ion radius of Na<sup>+</sup> than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, leading to poor cycling stability and unsatisfying rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0003_R_d30669543e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the higher negative redox potential of Na compared with that of Li makes it much more difficult to find desirable electrode materials for SIBs. Therefore, developing and preparing suitable anode materials of SIBs with high performance for practical applications is highly desirable, yet challenging.</p>
         
         <p>Transition-metal sulfides (TMSs) (e.g., Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0004_R_d30669543e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0005_R_d30669543e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> FeS<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0006_R_d30669543e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0007_R_d30669543e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoS<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0008_R_d30669543e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano">8</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0009_R_d30669543e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0010_R_d30669543e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0011_R_d30669543e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Energy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0012_R_d30669543e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0013_R_d30669543e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and CoS)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0014_R_d30669543e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0015_R_d30669543e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been intensively investigated as anode materials of SIBs because of their pronouncedly high theoretical capacities based on conversion reaction or even alloy reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0004_R_d30669543e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0016_R_d30669543e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Fiber Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, iron sulfides have attracted increasing attention as anodes for SIBs due to the high sodium storage capacity (e.g., 610&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S), high voltage plateaus, low cost, high abundance, and eco-friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0017_R_d30669543e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has been demonstrated that iron sulfides undergo an intercalation-to-conversion reaction sequence upon sodiation during the initial discharge cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0018_R_d30669543e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the sodiation/desodiation process is inevitably accompanied by serious volume variations caused by the conversion reaction, which leads to the severe destruction of the electrode structure and hence rapid capacity loss and deteriorative cycle lifetime.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0019_R_d30669543e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To solve this problem, Chen et&nbsp;al. adjusted the discharge cut-off voltage to 0.8&nbsp;V to avoid the huge volume variations caused by the conversion reaction, in which only the interaction reaction happened during the discharge and charge processes. To mitigate the significant volume variations resulting from the conversion reaction, Chen et&nbsp;al. opted to adjust the discharge cut-off voltage to 0.8&nbsp;V, allowing only the interaction reaction to occur during both the discharge and charge processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0020_R_d30669543e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the FeS<sub>2</sub> electrode offered unprecedented long-term cyclability with a capacity retention of ≈90% over 20&nbsp;000&nbsp;cycles. However, this cut-off voltage modulation strategy leads to an inevitable capacity loss due to the lack of a complete conversion reaction, which predominantly contributes to the overall capacity of the intercalation-to-conversion reaction. Moreover, the iron sulfide-based anodes also face the issues of poor electrical conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0019_R_d30669543e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0021_R_d30669543e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Alloys Compd.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the sluggish reaction kinetics in iron sulfide electrode materials, the irreversible surface-limited redox reaction always occurs in bulk electrode materials, thus impeding successive conversion cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0022_R_d30669543e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can lead to incomplete conversion reactions and will repeat the reaction in subsequent cycling, resulting in forming a thick insulating layer, and thus leading to serious capacity decay.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0023_R_d30669543e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address these problems, some feasible solutions have been proposed including morphology modification, atomic-level engineering, and iron sulfide composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0024_R_d30669543e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, reducing the size of electrode material to nanoscale combined with the carbon modification has been identified as one of most effective and promising electrode configurations, which could not only enhance electrical conductivity of the whole electrode, but also improve the reaction kinetics of the active materials, as well as alleviate structural degradation during the conversion reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0025_R_d30669543e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Environ. Sci.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, even though these strategies are effective to improve the electrochemical performance to a certain extent, the fabrication of iron sulfide anodes simultaneously delivering large capacity, fast charging capability, and high cyclability is still a challenge, which is related to the evitable pulverization problems induced by volume expansion and incomplete conversion reaction during repeated sodiation and desodiation process.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, some reports demonstrated that the iron sulfide nanoparticles with quantum size (d&lt;10&nbsp;nm) could overcome the kinetic and thermodynamic limitations of the Na─S conversion reaction, realizing the highly reversible, and efficient conversion reaction to offer higher specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0026_R_d30669543e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small">26</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0027_R_d30669543e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The quantum size comparable to or smaller than the diffusion lengths of Fe (L<sub>D</sub>≈10<sup>−18</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2&nbsp;</sup>s<sup>−1</sup> at room temperature) in iron sulfides can yield thermodynamically reversible nanodomains of converted Fe<sup>0</sup> metal and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>S conversion products during cation exchange.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0026_R_d30669543e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the short diffusion distances of Fe, the desired particle size of iron sulfides &lt;10&nbsp;nm could enable a relatively complete conversion reaction. For example, Choi et al. designed Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles with an average size of 10&nbsp;nm encapsulated in carbon, which delivered a high rate capacity (550&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.7&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and superior high and stable capacity (447&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.18&nbsp;A&nbsp; g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000&nbsp;cycles) in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0026_R_d30669543e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Given the above analysis, it is highly expected to realize high-performance iron sulfide anodes by combining the ultrafine iron sulfides particles with quantum size and conductive carbonaceous materials to achieve high reversible capacity, excellent rate capability, and superior long-term&nbsp;cyclability in SIBs simultaneously.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we designed and synthesized ultrathin carbon-coated Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S (Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C) nanoplates by a facile coating-carbonation-sulfidation strategy. The size of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates with a carbon layer could be reduced to ≈ 5&nbsp;nm. When evaluated as an anode for SIBs, an ultrafine pulverization phenomenon of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C electrode was unexpectedly observed during the initial discharge cycle. Impressively, instead of suffering from serious capacity fading, the pulverized Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C electrode simultaneously exhibited high capacity, superior rate capability, and ultralong cycling lifetime. Ex situ HRTEM analysis revealed that the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates were pulverized into small particles with quantum size (≈5&nbsp;nm) and tightly embedded in the carbon matrix. More impressively, there was no discernible aggregation phenomenon was detected in subsequent cycles. In situ XRD and Raman demonstrated that the pulverized nanoparticles could overcome the significant kinetic and thermodynamic limitations of the conversion reaction, realizing the highly reversible conversion reaction of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S. Moreover, Density functional theory (DFT) calculations further deciphered that this ultrafine pulverization of carbon-coated Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S nanoplates could not only significantly reduce both the adsorbed energies and diffusion energy barriers of Na atom but also effectively prevent the aggregation of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S particles by strengthening the adsorption between pulverized Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S nanoparticles and carbon. As a result, benefiting from the unique ultrafine pulverization, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C anode exhibited a high reversible capacity of 610&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, superb rate capability of 427.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and ultralong cycling stability over 2500&nbsp;cycles at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work holds a meaningful insight into the pulverization phenomenon in electrode study.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202312190-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202312190-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1a</a> schematically illustrates the fabrication process of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates. that involves polydopamine coating, carbonization, and sulfidation treatment. The phase of FeOOH, intermediate products (Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C), and Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD patterns of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information) showed all the diffraction peaks, which could be indexed to Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S (Card No. 29–0726). The TEM images of FeOOH and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C were displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), and both of them were plate-like nanoparticles. The resulting samples at sulfidation temperatures of 400 and 500&nbsp;°C were detonated as Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 and Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500, respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1b</a> displayes the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 nanoplates and the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplate (the insert in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1b</a>). It can be seen that the plate-like structure was well preserved after sulfidation. Besides, the TEM image of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 nanoplates was recorded in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), and its morphology exhibited little structural distortion compared with that of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 nanoplates. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1c,d</a> showed the high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images of the as-prepared Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400, and it showed the porous nanoplates with a thickness of ≈5.1&nbsp;nm and an inplane extension as long as 50–250&nbsp;nm. Besides, the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) exhibited that the thickness of the carbon layer was ≈2.7&nbsp;nm, and a characteristic interplanar distance of 0.263&nbsp;nm was consistent with the inter-plane distance of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S (2011) plane<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0028_R_d30669543e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1e,f</a>). The STEM energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images revealed the existence of an N-doped carbon layer and the homogenous distribution of N, C, S, and Fe elements in the composite (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0001">1g,g1-5</a>). The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements revealed that Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites had large Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface areas. The surface area of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites increased from 85.4 to 116.6&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 and Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400, respectively, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Furthermore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to characterize the surface chemical composition of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composite. The existence of C, Fe, and S elements was confirmed in the survey spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution C 1s XPS&nbsp;spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> (Supporting Information) could be deconvoluted into three peaks at 284.6, 286.2, and 288.7&nbsp;eV, which were attributed to the C─C, C─O, and O─C═O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0029_R_d30669543e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Acta Metall. Sin.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Fe 2p XPS&nbsp;spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a>, Supporting Information), two peaks located at 711.9 and 726.2&nbsp;eV were observed, which were ascribed to the Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. As seen from the S 2p XPS spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5d</a> (Supporting Information), the peak at 167.9&nbsp;eV could be assigned to SO<i><sub>x</sub></i> due to the surface oxidation, and peaks at 164.2 and 163.1&nbsp;eV are related to the S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> in the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S, respectively. A C─S peak appeared at ≈163.5&nbsp;eV, demonstrating the chemical bonding between the carbon shell and the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S core shell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0030_R_d30669543e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Raman spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the two peaks at 1354 and 1588&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> could be assigned to the D and G bands of carbon materials, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0031_R_d30669543e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbon">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratios of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 and Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 were 0.93 and 0.89, respectively, indicating a low graphitization of the carbon layer.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202312190-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44ba1ed5-36fb-4add-a512-372b90dc55e7/smll202312190-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/44ba1ed5-36fb-4add-a512-372b90dc55e7/smll202312190-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e20541c3-8d7a-48a3-b000-3a1f11339f8d/smll202312190-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44ba1ed5-36fb-4add-a512-372b90dc55e7/smll202312190-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202312190-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202312190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the synthetic process for Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates and microstructure characterization of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400. a) Schematic illustration of the preparation process of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates. b,f,g) TEM image of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400. c and d) STEM image of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 composite. f) FESEM image and e,f) TEM image of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 composite. g and g<sub>1-5</sub>) STEM-EDS mapping images of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 composite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performances of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites as SIBs anodes were tested, and the results were presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2a</a> showes cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode for the first three cycles in a potential window of 0.01–3&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. In the 1<sup>st</sup> cathodic scan, a weak reduction peak at ≈1.56&nbsp;V could be associated with the sodiation process of generation of the Na-rich phase (Na<i><sub>y</sub></i>Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0021_R_d30669543e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Alloys Compd.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A weak peak at 1.2&nbsp;V, a sharp cathodic peak at 0.9&nbsp;V, and a broad peak at 0.64 could be assigned to the reaction of Na and Na<i><sub>y</sub></i>Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S accompanied by the formation of Fe, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na-rich phases depending on the quantity of Na transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0032_R_d30669543e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, these three peaks also involved the contribution from the decomposition of electrolyte and formation process of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film because their intensities exhibited remarkable decay in the next cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0005_R_d30669543e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, a broad peak centered at 0.32&nbsp;V could be attributed to the conversion reaction to form Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0033_R_d30669543e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci. China Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the clear cathodic peak ≈0.04&nbsp;V was ascribed to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into carbon. During the first anodic scan, a sharp peak at 0.073&nbsp;V was attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from carbon materials, which was similar to the previous works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0034_R_d30669543e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - RSC Adv.">34</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0035_R_d30669543e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbon">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, three anodic peaks centered at 1.35, 1.53, and 1.81&nbsp;V might be related to the reverse conversion reaction between metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix with the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0036_R_d30669543e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">36</a>-<a href="#smll202312190-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0038_R_d30669543e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, two broad peaks at ≈2.1 and 2.6&nbsp;V could be ascribed to the multiple-step Na<sup>+</sup> desodiation from Na<sub>2</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> with the generation of Na<sub>2-n</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> and FeS<i><sub>x</sub></i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0039_R_d30669543e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the second cycle onward, there was no obvious loss of peak area in the successive cycles, indicating the highly reversible electrochemical reaction in the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S composite after the first sodiation/desodiation process. Besides, CV curves of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 were also recorded, showing a similar profile to that of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The charge/discharge profiles of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C from the 1st to 10<sup>th</sup> cycle at 0. 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2b</a>. They were almost overlapped, suggesting a stable electrochemical reversibility of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C electrode after the initial discharge process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2c</a> exhibits the cyclic performance of as-prepared Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites. The initial charge/discharge capacity of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 anode was 610.5 and 779&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 77.5%. The capacity loss was primarily attributed to the irreversible formation of the SEI films. The CE rapidly increased to ≈95.4% in the second cycle and could be maintained at a high level of &gt;98% in the following cycles. The Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 anode delivered a reversible capacity of 690.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200&nbsp;cycles, respectively, which was higher than that of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 anode (560&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200&nbsp;cycles). Moreover, the rate capabilities of both anode materials were also studied at a sequence of current densities from 0.3 to 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and further sequentially decreased to 0.3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2d</a>, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 delivered discharge capacities of 523.4, 503.1, 484.8, 453.2, 411.3, 372.1, and 327.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at specific currents of 0.3, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When tested at the same current densities, the rate capacities of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 sample were 619.2, 589.8, 587.8, 564.6, 520.9, 477.9, and 427.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Upon returning the current density to 0.3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 sample still delivered a discharge capacity of ≈626.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, slightly higher than the initial discharge capacity, indicating stable SEI formation and reduced the dissolution of polysulfide during the rate capability test.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0040_R_d30669543e1054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparatively, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 electrode delivered a relatively low rate capacity, which could be mainly attributed to slower charge transfer kinetics, as discussed later. The discharge/charge profiles at different rates were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2e</a>. The flat section sharply decreased at stepwise current densities, indicating that the adsorption of Na ions at the active site became much faster. In other words, the pulverization of active materials could shorten the diffusion distance of Na ions and improve the diffusion rate. The sufficient active sites could enhance the sodium storage capacity by capturing more Na ions and improving surface-controlled pseudocapacitive effect. To assess the rate and long-term cycle performance of our Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S composites, previously reported iron sulfide anodes were examined for comparison, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). The long-term cycling performance of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C samples was further evaluated at a large current density of 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0002">2g</a>. All of them exhibited excellent cycle life up to 2500&nbsp;cycles without obvious capacity fading. The reversible capacity of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 reached 455.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle and was stabilized at 377.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500&nbsp;cycles with a capacity retention of 82.8%, which was significantly higher than that of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 (305.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2500&nbsp;cycles with a capacity retention of 76.4%).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202312190-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6547fb63-ae20-484f-a461-8a8c8b207ace/smll202312190-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6547fb63-ae20-484f-a461-8a8c8b207ace/smll202312190-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f4c100b1-7225-4d23-b062-a103f5b81abe/smll202312190-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6547fb63-ae20-484f-a461-8a8c8b207ace/smll202312190-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202312190-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202312190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage performance of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites. a) CV curves of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charge–discharge profiles of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C at different cycles (from selected cycles as indicted); c) Cycle performance of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; d) Rate performance of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites; e) Charge–discharge profiles of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C at different current densities; f) Comparison of rate performance between Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 and as-reported iron sulfide anodes for SIBs; g) Long-term cycling performance of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composites at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further visually illustrate the phase transition, microstructure, and morphology evolution during the sodiation/desodiation process, ex situ TEM was carried out to monitor the cycling process of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400. TEM, HRTEM, SAED pattern, and STEM-EDS mapping images of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode at first discharged state (0.01&nbsp;V) and charged state (3.0&nbsp;V) in the cut-off range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V were presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3</a>. Unexpectedly, the ultrafine pulverization of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates happened after the first discharge cycle. It could be seen that the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C nanoplates disappeared and pulverized into ultrafine nanoparticles with quantum size (≈5&nbsp;nm), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3a,b</a>. Additionally, the obvious d-spacings of 0.308 and 0.201&nbsp;nm in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3c</a> could be assigned to the (200) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S and (110) plane of Fe, respectively. The corresponding SAED pattern was well indexed to the diffraction patterns of the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe phases (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3d</a>), suggesting a deep conversion reaction. The STEM-EDS mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3e</a> indicate the existence of Fe and S elements, verifying a significant pulverization phenomenon. When charging the cell back to 3.0&nbsp;V, the TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3f,g</a>) reveal that aggregation of the ultrafine nanoparticles had not taken place. Noticed that there were no discernable changes in the size and morphology of charged product compared to that of the initial discharge product. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3h</a> shows the d-spacings of 0.213 and 0.272&nbsp;nm, which matched well with the (2022) and (2011) planes of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S, respectively, which demonstrated that the formation of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S and the high reversibility of the electrode. Besides, the corresponding SAED pattern was well indexed to the diffraction patterns of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3i</a>). The STEM-EDS mapping images suggested that the Fe and S elements of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S phase composite were uniformly distributed in the carbon matrix (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). To check the state of active material particles in the subsequent cycles, TEM images of the electrodes at full charge state after 100 and 200&nbsp;cycles were recorded in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3j–m</a>, respectively. There was no discernable aggregation observed, indicating the superior stability of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S electrode after ultrafine pulverization. In addition, a similar pulverization phenomenon was also observed in the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-500 anode after full discharge, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). The average sizes of the pulverized particles were ≈9&nbsp;nm, which was a little larger than that of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 anode, which could be related to the different degree of crystallinity, graphitization of carbon layer, and structural distortion resulting from higher temperature calcination. As revealed from the above analysis, the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C composite after pulverization remained granular without aggregation during the subsequent discharge–charge process. that could be the primary reason for the outstanding sodium storage performance. The schematic illustration of the particle pulverization processes was summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202312190-fig-0003">3n</a>. We have observed that the reaction mechanism involved differs from the common ones. Typically, the pulverization of electrode materials often leads to material aggregation and subsequent capacity decay. In the case of this particular phenomenon, the paper will provide further insights through theoretical calculations. Additionally, it is noteworthy that the generation of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S is observed after complete charging, which deviates from previous studies and suggests an incomplete reversibility. This further confirms that the pulverization of quantum dots can be attributed to a nanoparticle size comparable to or smaller than the diffusion length of Fe during cation exchange, resulting in thermodynamically reversible nanodomains of converted Fe metal, and Na<sub>2</sub>S conversion products.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202312190-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7fa2f2f6-04d5-415a-bcc1-2b6ef0cc9dac/smll202312190-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7fa2f2f6-04d5-415a-bcc1-2b6ef0cc9dac/smll202312190-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95a730ff-0f7b-48fc-9fa0-63479eb6d5b1/smll202312190-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7fa2f2f6-04d5-415a-bcc1-2b6ef0cc9dac/smll202312190-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202312190-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202312190" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructure characterization of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode during cycling and schematic representation of cycling-dependent evolution of the microstructure of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode; a–e) TEM, HRTEM, SAED pattern, and STEM-EDS mapping images of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode at fully discharged state (0.01&nbsp;V); f–i) TEM, HRTEM, HRTEM, and SAED pattern of the Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode at fully charged state (3&nbsp;V); j,k) TEM and HRTEM images of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode after 100&nbsp;cycles; l,m) TEM and HRTEM images of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 electrode after 200&nbsp;cycles; n) Schematic illustration of the particle pulverization processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further reveal the voltage-dependent phase transformation behavior of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@−400 during the first discharge/charge process, in situ XRD was carried out and the results were presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202312190-fig-0004">4a,b</a>. It was noted that the two peaks located at 38.5° and 34.7° were ascribed to aluminum foil and Be, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0017_R_d30669543e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0041_R_d30669543e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Apparently, the intensity of all the diffraction peaks of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S@C-400 visibly decreased during the discharge process. According to in situ XRD data, a four-stage process combined with insertion/conversion mechanisms was concluded, which agreed with previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0037_R_d30669543e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A">37</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0042_R_d30669543e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reaction processes during the first sodiation/desodiation process were depicted with color-coding for overall understanding. In the first stage, the peaks at 29.5° and 33.6° belonged to the (200) and (2011) facets of Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S, respectively, exhibiting a slight shift to a lower angle when it discharged from OCV to ≈0.8&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0032_R_d30669543e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#smll202312190-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0043_R_d30669543e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon could be assigned to the insertion of sodium ions into Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S and the formation of Na<i><sub>y</sub></i>Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S, which led to the increase in interplanar distances. In the second stage, the Na<i><sub>y</sub></i>Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S was eventually converted to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the peak intensities of (200) and (2011) gradually weakened and disappeared when it was discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V. In the third stage, a new peak appeared at 30.8° when it was recharged to ≈0.8&nbsp;V, which could be attributed to the (221) planes of NaFeS<sub>2</sub>, indicating the formation of the Na-rich phase (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS<i><sub>y</sub></i>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202312190-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202312190-bib-0044_R_d30669543e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the fourth stage, when it charged to 3&nbsp;V, the XRD patterns could be ascribed to the formation of Na<sub>2-</sub><i><sub>n</sub></i>FeS<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S. and the weak intensities of diffraction peaks could be attributed to the ultrafine pulverization phenomenon that led to a reduction in the size of the electrode material and the degree of crystallinity. The related multi-steps sodiation–desodiation reaction mechanism can be described as the following equations:

            <ol start="1" class="">
               
               <li>
                  <p>Stage I (intercalation)

                     </p><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202312190-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                                       <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>

                                       <mi>ye</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">−</mo>

                  <p></p>
